6.3
Median
Finding the median accurately in a diï¬€erentially private manner is challenging
due to the high â€œglobal sensitivityâ€: there are two datasets that diï¬€er by one ele-
ment but having a completely diï¬€erent median. Nevertheless, for many instances,
their â€œlocal sensitivityâ€ are small. Nissim et al. [20] showed that in general, by
adding noise proportional to the â€œsmooth sensitivityâ€ of the database instance,
instead of the global sensitivity, can also ensure diï¬€erential privacy. They also
gave an Î˜(n2) algorithm that ï¬nd the smooth sensitivity w.r.t. median.
Our mechanism outputs the sorted sequence diï¬€erentially privately, and thus
naturally gives the median. Compare to the smooth sensitivity-based mechanism,
our mechanism provides more information in the sense that it outputs the whole
sorted sequence. Furthermore, our mechanism can be eï¬ƒciently carried out in
O(n log n) time.
We conduct experiments on synthetic datasets of size 129 to compare the
accuracy of both mechanisms. The experiments are conducted for diï¬€erent local
sensitivity and diï¬€erent Ïµ values. To construct a dataset with a particular local
sensitivity, 66 random numbers are generated with the exponential distribution
and then scaled to the unit interval. The dataset contains the 66 random numbers
and 63 ones. Fig. 10(a) and 10(b) shows the noise level with diï¬€erent Ïµ on datasets
that has a local sensitivity of 0.1 and 0.3.
When the local sensitivity of the median is high, our mechanism tends to
provide a better result. In addition, our mechanism performs well under higher
requirement of security: when the Ïµ is smaller, the accuracy of our mechanism
decreases slower than the smooth sensitivity-based method.

7
Discussion and Future Work
7.1
Hybrid Method
The proposed mechanism can be viewed as the publishing of a â€œequi-depthâ€ his-
togram, where the â€œdepthâ€ is the group size. Potentially, our proposed method

Adaptive Diï¬€erentially Private Histogram of Low-Dimensional Data
177
and equi-width histogram could complement each other, by alternatively publish-
ing one after another. For example, we can ï¬rst apply an equi-width histogram
to get a coarse distribution of the data, followed by our method for a â€œzoom-
inâ€ view. Alternatively, we can apply equi-width histogram after our method to
â€œbreakâ€ the stepping eï¬€ect of isotonic regression.
7.2
Eï¬€ect of Dimension
We rely on a locality-preserving mapping T to extend the mechanism to higher
dimension. Although it is shown that the distant between two d dimensional
points x, y is preserved and bounded by c(âˆ¥T (x) âˆ’T (y)âˆ¥)1/d (see Section 2.3),
the curse-of-dimensionality is still in play in higher dimension. Firstly, to our
best knowledge, there is no known eï¬ƒcient constructions for dimensions higher
than 3. Secondly, the exponential factor 1/d ampliï¬es the error: for example, Fig.
5 shows that our scheme can reduce the error of âˆ¥T (x)âˆ’T (y)âˆ¥to 0.005, where y
is the reconstructed point for x. When d is 2, we can have âˆ¥x âˆ’yâˆ¥2 bounded by
0.07c; when d is 3, the bound is increased to 0.17c. We are unable to verify the
performance in higher dimension due to the lack of eï¬ƒcient construction, and
leave the accurate extension to higher dimensional data as future work.
8
Related Work
There are extensive works on privacy-preserving data publishing. The recent
survey by Fung et al. [8] gives a comprehensive overview on various notions, for
example, k-anonymity [26], â„“-diversity [15], and the recently proposed concept
of diï¬€erential privacy [5].
Xiao et al. [28] proposed a mechanism of adding Laplace noise to the co-
eï¬ƒcients of a wavelet transformation of an equi-width histogram. The noisy
wavelet coeï¬ƒcients are then published, from which range queries can be an-
swered. Essentially, what being published is a series of equi-width histograms
for diï¬€erent bin-widths where the noise added to the histograms of larger bin-
width are smaller. A range query can then be decomposed and answered from
the histograms series diï¬€erent scales.
Isotonic regression has been used to improve a diï¬€erentially private query re-
sult. Hay et al. [11] proposed a method that employs isotonic regression to boost
accuracy, but in a way diï¬€erent from our mechanism. They consider publishing
unattributed histogram, which is the (unordered) multi-set of the frequencies of
a histogram. As the frequencies are unattributed (i.e. order of appearance is ir-
relevant), they proposed publishing the sorted frequencies and later employing
isotonic regression to improve accuracy.
Machanavajjhala et al. [16] proposed a 2D dataset publishing method that
can handle the sparse data in 2D equi-width histogram. To mitigate the sparse
data, their method shrinks the sparse blocks by examining publicly available
data such as a previously release of similar data. They demonstrate this idea
on the commuting patterns of the population of the United States, which is a

178
C. Fang and E.-C. Chang
real-life sparse 2D map in large domain. As their method partitions the space
based on a previously released data, we consider the partition as pre-determined
partition and is not adaptive to the publishing dataset.
The median is an important statistic, and a diï¬€erentially private median ï¬nd-
ing process can be useful in many constructions, such as in pointset spatial de-
composition [4,23]. However, ï¬nding the median diï¬€erentially privately is not
easy due to the large global sensitivity. Nissim et al. [20] introduced the notion
of smooth sensitivity and proposed an accurate mechanism with Î˜(n2) running
time.
9
Conclusion
Our mechanism is very simple from the publisherâ€™s point of view. The publisher
just has to sort the points, group consecutive values, add Laplace noise and
publish the noisy data. There is also minimal tuning to be carried out by the
publisher. The main design decision is the choice of the group size k, which can
be determined using our proposed noise models, and the locality-preserving map-
ping for which the classic Hilbert curve suï¬ƒces to attain high accuracy. Through
empirical studies, we have shown that the published raw data contain rich infor-
mation for the public to harvest, and provide high accuracy even for usages like
median-ï¬nding and range-searching that our mechanism is not initially designed
for.

PRISM â€“ Privacy-Preserving Search in MapReduce
Erik-Oliver Blass1, Roberto Di Pietro2, Reï¬k Molva3, and Melek Â¨Onen3
1 Northeastern University, Boston, MA
2 Universit`a di Roma Tre, Rome, Italy
3 EURECOM, Sophia Antipolis, France
Abstract. We present PRISM, a privacy-preserving scheme for word search in
cloud computing. In the face of a curious cloud provider, the main challenge is
to design a scheme that achieves privacy while preserving the efï¬ciency of cloud
computing. Solutions from related research, like encrypted keyword search or
Private Information Retrieval (PIR), fall short of meeting real-world cloud re-
quirements and are impractical. PRISMâ€™s idea is to transform the problem of
word search into a set of parallel instances of PIR on small datasets. Each PIR
instance on a small dataset is efï¬ciently solved by a node in the cloud during the
â€œMapâ€ phase of MapReduce. Outcomes of map computations are then aggregated
during the â€œReduceâ€ phase. Due to the linearity of PRISM, the simple aggrega-
tion of map results yields the ï¬nal output of the word search operation. We have
implemented PRISM on Hadoop MapReduce and evaluated its efï¬ciency using
real-world DNS logs. PRISMâ€™s overhead over non-private search is only 11%.
Thus, PRISM offers privacy-preserving search that meets cloud computing efï¬-
ciency requirements. Moreover, PRISM is compatible with standard MapReduce,
not requiring any change to the interface or infrastructure.
1
Introduction
Today, users take advantage of public clouds operated by large companies like Google
or Amazon. Instead of setting up and maintaining their own data centers, users reduce
their costs by outsourcing both storage and processing to a cloud. One prominent exam-
ple allowing cloud-based storage and processing is Hadoop MapReduce [3], a variant
of Googleâ€™s MapReduce system [17]. Hadoop MapReduce is widely used, and public
MapReduce clouds are offered by companies such as Amazon [2, 25].
The advantages of cloud computing unfortunately come with a high cost in terms of
new security and privacy exposures. Apart from classical security challenges of shared
services, outsourcing of data storage and processing raises new challenges in the face of
potentially malicious cloud providers. Privacy of outsourced data appears to be a major
requirement in this context. Some regulations are already provisioned as to the privacy
protection of outsourced governmental documents [11, 12, 18]: these regulations usu-
ally aim at assuring privacy against curious clouds or against clouds with data centers
located in â€œrogueâ€ countries or with insufï¬cient security guarantees; they also are de-
ï¬ned to avoid data leakage in case of operational failures in the cloud. Along these lines,
there is also a raising corporate concern about the privacy of sensitive business data
stored in the cloud [14]. Although cloud providers thrive to meet the increased privacy
S. Fischer-HÂ¨ubner and M. Wright (Eds.): PETS 2012, LNCS 7384, pp. 180â€“200, 2012.
câƒSpringer-Verlag Berlin Heidelberg 2012

PRISM â€“ Privacy-Preserving Search in MapReduce
181
demand by certifying their services [24], malicious insiders have still been identiï¬ed as
one of the top threats in cloud computing [15].
While encryption of outsourced data by the users seems to be a viable protection
against most privacy problems, traditional data encryption does not suit the require-
ments of cloud computing: the cloud not only serves as high capacity memory, but is
also involved in data processing such as statistical data analysis, log analysis, index-
ing, data mining, and searching [25]. However, data processing performed by the cloud
would not be feasible or would be inefï¬cient with encrypted data.
Among data processing primitives, word search, i.e., verifying, whether a certain
word is part of a dataset, is not only one of the most fundamental operations, but sur-
prisingly also one of the most demanded applications in, e.g., MapReduce cloud com-
puting [25]. Related work on search in encrypted data, e.g., Boneh et al. [6], Ogata
and Kurosawa [31], falls short of meeting cloud computing privacy and performance
requirements. These techniques are impractical as they are designed for centralized ex-
ecution models that are incompatible with todayâ€™s highly parallel cloud architectures.
In this paper, we present PRISM, a new scheme for privacy-preserving and efï¬cient
word search for MapReduce clouds. PRISM pursues two speciï¬c objectives: 1.) pri-
vacy against potentially malicious cloud providers and 2.) high efï¬ciency through the
integration of security mechanisms with the operations performed in the cloud.
In order to achieve efï¬ciency, PRISM takes advantage of the inherent parallelization
akin to cloud computing: the word search problem on a very large encrypted dataset is
partitioned into several instances of word search in small datasets that are executed in
parallel (â€œMapâ€ phase). The individual word search operations performed in the cloud
yield a result amenable to straightforward aggregation in the ultimate phase (â€œReduceâ€
phase) of the word search operation. The word search operation builds on a Private
Information Retrieval (PIR) [29] technique which is extended in order to generate in-
termediate search results that are still encrypted and that can be combined through linear
operations to yield the global result of the word search over the entire dataset.
Summarizing our contributions, PRISM:
â€¢ is suited to cloud computing: PRISM is the ï¬rst privacy-preservingsearch scheme
suited to cloud computing; it brings together storage and search privacy with high per-
formance by leveraging the efï¬ciency of the MapReduce paradigm. PRISM is paral-
lelizable and also allows efï¬cient combination of individual results. Its efï¬ciency has
been evaluated through searching in DNS logs provided by an Internet Service Provider.
Although PRISMâ€™s overhead within the core Map function is large compared to non-
privacy-preserving search (factor of 9), the total system overhead is only 11%.
â€¢ preserves privacy in the face of potentially malicious cloud providers: PRISM
allows carrying out these critical operations in the cloud without trusting the cloud.
â€¢ is compatible to standard MapReduce: PRISM only requires a standard MapRe-
duce interface without modiï¬cations in the underlying system. PRISM can thus be in-
tegrated on any cloud that provides a standard MapReduce interface such as Amazon.
â€¢ provides ï¬‚exible search: In contrast to traditional encrypted keyword search tech-
niques, PRISM is not limited to searching for a ï¬xed set of predetermined keywords to
be known in advance, but offers ï¬‚exible search for any words.

182
E.-O. Blass et al.
2
Problem Statement and Adversary Model
Throughout this paper, we will use an application example to motivate our work. In-
spired by recent events [30], we envision a data retention scenario. Due to regulatory
matters, a small, residential Internet Service Provider (ISP) must retain logs of client
accesses. Due to the sheer amount of data to retain, the ISP outsources logï¬les to the
cloud. Files are encrypted as they contain sensitive data. Still, e.g., law enforcement
authorities will contact the ISP to search for words (strings, text, ...) in outsourced ï¬les.
More concretely, assume service provider U (the cloud â€œuserâ€) providing DNS ser-
vices to clients. U logs each clientâ€™s access, i.e., U logs the tuple (timestamp, client ID,
hostname queried). Due to the large amount of log data and cost reasons, U outsources
its logï¬les into a cloud. Regularly, say each day i, U creates a new logï¬le Li. At the end
of a longer period, U wants to (or is forced to) ï¬nd out, whether there was an interest
in a suspicious host w. So, U checks, at which day, i.e., in which logï¬les Li, word w
occurs. U queries the cloud for w, and the cloud responses with an answer R telling U
which of the Li contain(s) w.
Note that U does not know in advance which word w it has to search for. This auto-
matically disqualiï¬es protocols for predeï¬ned keyword search, such as PEKS [6] and
derivatives. Also, data retention regulations require outsourced data to be fully recover-
able; storing only digests of data in the cloud, e.g., hash values, is insufï¬cient.
The cloud is assumed to be untrusted, more precisely semi-honest (â€œhonest-but-
curiousâ€). Regulatory matters imply that the cloud must not learn any information about
the content it hosts and search queries performed. This implies both, the encryption of
data by U before outsourcing it to the cloud and â€œobliviouslyâ€ processing queries on
encrypted data by the cloud.
Before we formalize our privacy requirements, we ï¬rst deï¬ne the main components
for a cloud word search scheme.
Deï¬nition 1 (Cloud Word Search). Let L denote a sequence of ï¬les L := {L1, . . . ,
Lm} and Î£ the set of possible words. Each ï¬le Li consists of a sequence of words
Li := {w(i,1), w(i,2), . . . , w(i,|Li|)}, w(i,j) âˆˆÎ£.
A cloud word search scheme comprises the following algorithms:
1. KeyGen(s): using a security parameter s, this algorithm outputs a secret S.
2. Encrypt(S, L): uses the secret S to encrypt the content of ï¬les Li âˆˆL and outputs
the set of resulting encryptions E := {EL1, . . . , ELm}. Here, ELi denotes the
encryption of ï¬le Li.
3. Upload(E): uploads E to the cloud.
4. PrepareQuery(S, w): takes S, the word w to search for, and produces a query Q.
5. Process(E, Q): with encryptions E and query Q, this algorithm produces result R.
6. Decode(S, R, w): taking result R, secret S, and word w, this algorithm outputs
the set of indices I := {i1, . . . , ir} such that âˆ€i âˆˆI : Li âˆˆL âˆ§w âˆˆLi, if
R = Process(E, Q) with Q = PrepareQuery(S, w), and E = Encrypt(S, L).
The basic interaction between user U and the cloud can be summarized as follows:
ï¬rst, user U encrypts and uploads ï¬les. Then, U prepares a search query Q for word
w and sends Q to the cloud. The cloud processes this query using algorithm Process

PRISM â€“ Privacy-Preserving Search in MapReduce
183
and produces output R. This output is sent back to U. Using output R and another
algorithm Decode, U can compute the list of ï¬les containing w. While describing
PRISMâ€™s details later in Section 4, we will show how they map to these algorithms.
Note that, in
a
cloud setting, U
executes KeyGen, Encrypt,
Upload,
PrepareQuery, and Decode, while the cloud executes Process. The idea is that algo-
rithms KeyGen, Encrypt, Upload, PrepareQuery, and Decode are computationally
very â€œlightweightâ€ for U compared to Process. The main computational burden lies on
the cloud side.
Privacy Requirements
Intuitively, our application demands for two main types of privacy. The cloud (now
called â€œadversary Aâ€) must neither be able to infer any details about stored ï¬les nor
learn details about Uâ€™s queries and results delivered back to U. This implies not only
the secrecy or conï¬dentiality of the content, but also the inability to compute statistics
on the content. Informally, in our setting:
â€“ given E, A must not learn the content of L and must not discover whether ï¬les Li
contain a word w, e.g., multiple times;
â€“ given a set of queries {Qi}, A must not learn the words {wi} U is looking for and
must not discover whether the same word is queried multiple times;
â€“ given the result Ri of a query Qi, U must not learn which ï¬le(s) contain the word
corresponding to this speciï¬c query Wi.
Instead, the adversary should only learn â€œtrivialâ€ properties, such as the total number of
ï¬les, the ï¬le size, and the total number of queries. Along the same lines as traditional
indistinguishability [23], A should not be able to infer any additional information from
encrypted ï¬les, queries, and results. We formally deï¬ne privacy for a cloud word search
scheme using a game between adversary A (the cloud) and a challenger (user U).
Deï¬nition 2 (Privacy). Let W denote a sequence of words W := {w1, . . . , wn}. The
game GAME is played as follows.
1. The challenger executes KeyGen(s) to derive secret S.
2. A selects a distinct pair of sequences of ï¬les and words (L0, W0) and (L1, W1),
where |L0| = |L1|, âˆ€(L0
i âˆˆL0, L1
i âˆˆL1) : |L0
i | = |L1
i |, and |W0| = |W1|.
A sends (L0, W0) and (L1, W1) to the challenger.
3. The challenger randomly selects b âˆˆ{0, 1} and
â€“ executes Encrypt(S, Lb), i.e., the challenger computes encrypted ï¬les Eb =
{E(S, Li)|Li âˆˆLb}.
â€“ executes Upload(Eb) to send encrypted ï¬les back to A.
â€“ executes PrepareQuery(S, w) for each w in Wb. This results in the sequence
of queries Qb := {Q1, . . . , Q|Wb|} that the challenger also sends to A.
4. A outputs bâ€² âˆˆ{0, 1}. The outcome of GAME is â€œ1â€ iff bâ€² = b.

184
E.-O. Blass et al.
A cloud word search scheme is called privacy-preserving iff
Pr(GAME(A) = 1) â‰¤1
2 + Ïµ(s)
for all probabilistic polynomial-time adversaries A. Here, Ïµ(s) is a negligible function,
Ïµ(s) <
1
P (s) for every polynomial P with sufï¬ciently large security parameter s.
The speciï¬cation of the (Li, Wi) in step 2. of Deï¬nition 2 reï¬‚ects the fact that A can
learn the total number of ï¬les, the size of each ï¬le, and the number of queries.
Limitations: We consider semi-honest clouds. Fully malicious clouds might perform
DoS-attacks or deviate from protocol execution. Similar to â€œreaction attacksâ€ [26], the
cloud might return garbage to U, to observe Uâ€™s reaction (e.g., sending the same query).
Although realistic, we leave such attacks for future work. Also, our privacy deï¬nition
does not capture trivial privacy properties, e.g., the size of outsourced ï¬les. Mitigation
strategies (e.g., padding ï¬les) might be contradictory to cloud efï¬ciency. We conjecture
that, for many applications, losing â€œtrivialâ€ privacy properties is acceptable.
3
Background
3.1
MapReduce
We target a system suited for the MapReduce [3] paradigm. We will now give a con-
densed overview of MapReduce, focusing on aspects necessary to understand PRISM.
Upload. A MapReduce cloud comprises a set of â€œslaveâ€ node computers and a â€œmasterâ€
computer. While U uploads ï¬les into the MapReduce cloud, each ï¬le is automatically
split into blocks called InputSplits. InputSplits have a ï¬xed size SInputSplit which is
a pre-conï¬gured system parameter. If SFile denotes the size of an uploaded ï¬le, the
number of InputSplits c computes to c =
SFile
SInputSplit . For each InputSplit, a workload
sharing algorithm selects a slave node and places the InputSplit on it.
In addition to data, the MapReduce also allows U to upload â€œoperationsâ€, i.e., com-
piled Java classes. These classes represent the implementation of three functions.
1.) Scan(INPUTSPLIT) â†’[(k, v)]), a functions that takes an InputSplit as an input,
parses it, i.e., scans it and generates a set of key-value pairs [(k, v)] out of it.
2.) Map(k, v) â†’[(kâ€², vâ€²)], a function that takes as an input a single key-value pair
(k, v) and outputs a set of â€œintermediateâ€ key-value pairs [(kâ€², vâ€²)].
3.) Reduce([(kâ€², vâ€²)]) â†’FILE, a function that takes as an input a set of intermediate
key-value pairs [(kâ€², vâ€²)] and writes arbitrary output into a ï¬le.
Uploaded Java classes are sent to all slave nodes storing an InputSplit.
Map Phase. After data and implementations have been uploaded, U speciï¬es one up-
loaded ï¬le and triggers MapReduce operations on that ï¬le. The ï¬rst phase of operation
is the â€œMapâ€ phase. Each slave node becomes a â€œmapperâ€ node. Each mapper executes
Uâ€™s Scan function on the InputSplit it stores locally. This generates a set of key-value
pairs on each mapper. Furthermore, the mapper node executes Uâ€™s Map function on
this generated key-value pairs to produce a set of intermediate key-value pairs.

PRISM â€“ Privacy-Preserving Search in MapReduce
185
Reduce Phase. MapReduce starts the â€œReduceâ€ phase. Slave nodes are scheduled to
become â€œreducersâ€. For each of the intermediate pairs (kâ€², vâ€²), MapReduce selects a
reducer and sends (kâ€², vâ€²) to this reducer. MapReduce selects the same reducer for all
pairs (kâ€², vâ€²) having the same key. Each reducer executes Uâ€™s reduce function on its set
of intermediate key-value pairs and writes the output to a ï¬le. This ï¬le is sent to U.
3.2
Trapdoor Group Private Information Retrieval
PIR allows a user to retrieve data from a server without revealing which data is re-
trieved. For PRISM, we make use of a simple and efï¬cient PIR mechanism as previ-
ously suggested by Trostle and Parrish [37]. As this mechanism is just a building block
for PRISM, we will only give a summary of its mode of operation and rationale.
Overview: Matrix M is a tÃ—t matrix of elements in ZN stored at a server. For example,
N = 2 for a binary matrix. User U is only interested in receiving elements of the kth
row in M, but the server must not learn k. The idea is now that U sends two â€œtypesâ€
of values to the server. For each row that U is not interested in, he sends a value of
the â€œï¬rstâ€ type. For the one row that U is interested in, he sends a single value of
the â€œsecondâ€ type. To prevent the server from distinguishing between the two types
of values, U blinds each value with a blinding factor b. This blinding factor can later
be removed by U. The server now performs simple additions with received values and
elements stored in M. The result is sent back to U who removes the blinding factor and
determines the values of the row of his interest.
Preparation: Assume U is interested in row k. U chooses a group Zp, with a prime p
of m bits. U also chooses a random b âˆˆZp and t random values ai âˆˆZp. Therewith, U
computes t values ei <
p
tÂ·(Nâˆ’1) such that: ek := 1 + ak Â· N and âˆ€i Ì¸= k : ei := ai Â· N.
Finally, U computes Î±i := b Â· ei mod p and sends the Î±i to the server. Other values
(p, m, b, {ei}, {ai}) remain secret. The server treats Î±i as large integers and performs
the following integer operations, i.e., without any modulo.
Server computation: Let u be the vector u := (Î±1, . . . , Î±t). The server computes the
matrix product v and sends it back to U,
v := (Î²1, . . . , Î²t) = u Â· M = (

Result analysis: Upon receipt, in order to â€œun-blindâ€ values, U computes the t inverse
values zi := Î²i Â· bâˆ’1 mod p. Now, U can conclude that zi mod N equals the ith
element of the kth row in M. Therewith, U has retrieved the t elements of the kth row
of M in a privacy-preserving fashion. Note the linearity for two Î²i and Î²â€²
i received
during different PIR runs: Î²i Â· bâˆ’1 + Î²â€²

vectors v, vâ€². We will later use this linearity during PRISMâ€™s reduce phase.
Security Rationale: Security and privacy of this protocol are based on the trapdoor
group assumption. With only knowledge of Î±i, but not secret trapdoor p, it is com-
putationally hard for the server to infer any information about low order bits, i.e., the
modulo of z or ei, cf., Trostle and Parrish [37].

186
E.-O. Blass et al.
Discussion: Again, we stress that this particular PIR scheme is an exchangeable build-
ing block. In general, any of the â€œtraditionalâ€ PIR techniques based on group homomor-
phic encryption [29, 33] is suited for use within PRISM. We have chosen Trostle and
Parrish [37] only due to the straightforward way to implement it (Section 6). Other PIR
schemes might reduce the (already small) overhead, but this is out of scope here.
4
PRISM Protocol
PRISM comprises three parts: upload of data into the cloud, the MapReduce search,
and the result analysis where the user decides whether the word has been found. We
will brieï¬‚y give an overview about each part.
1.) Upload. During upload, U encrypts each word (of a logï¬le) using symmetric encryp-
tion. Ciphertexts are stored in a ï¬le, and this ï¬le is sent to the MapReduce cloud. The
cloud automatically splits large ï¬les and distributes splits (InputSplits) among mapper
nodes. We use a standard blockcipher (AES) to perform ciphering of words. However,
to ensure privacy as of Deï¬nition 2, plaintext is modiï¬ed before encryption using a
â€œstateful cipherâ€ construction. Therewith, U can still search for some word w, but the
cloud cannot compute statistics about ciphertexts.
2.) Search. Eventually, U wants to search his encrypted ï¬les for some word w. There-
fore, U sends implementations of â€œalgorithmsâ€ for the map and reduce phases to the
MapReduce cloud, and the cloud executes these on uploaded data. For example, U sends
Java â€œ.classâ€ ï¬les for the mappers and Java â€œ.classâ€ ï¬les for reducer nodes. MapReduce
distributes these implementations to each mapper and reducer, respectively. PRISMâ€™s
rationale is to transform the word search problem into a set of small PIR instances.
To do so, each mapper, scanning through its locally stored InputSplit, creates a binary
matrix. Ciphertexts in the InputSplit are assigned to individual elements in that matrix.
If a ciphertext is present in an InputSplit, its corresponding element in the matrix is
set to either â€œ0â€ or â€œ1â€. Using private information retrieval techniques, PRISM can ex-
tract the value of a single element in the matrix with the mapper being totally oblivious
to which element is extracted. Consequently, U can specify which element to extract
in a privacy-preserving way. All mappers send their obliviously extracted elements as
key-value pairs to reducers. Reducers simply sum up received values and return sums
to U. Therewith, neither mappers nor reducers can learn any information about which
ciphertext U was interested in.
3.) Result analysis. Finally, U receives an encrypted sum for each of the originally up-
loaded ï¬les from reducers. U can decrypt them and decide which of the ï¬les contain w.
However, due to the probability of â€œcollisionsâ€ in matrices, i.e., two different cipher-
texts can be assigned to the same element, and due to ambiguities of received sums, Uâ€™s
decision whether w is inside some ï¬le might be wrong. Therefore, PRISM repeats the
above process in a total of q so called â€œroundsâ€. In each of the rounds, a new matrix is
generated, elements are set to â€œ1â€ or â€œ0â€ depending on the round number, and results
are returned as described. This reduces the probability of U making incorrect decisions.
Initialization: Before the actual uploading, initially, and only once, U has to execute
KeyGen. In PRISM, KeyGen outputs secret S := {K, N, p}, where |K|, |N|, |p| are

4.1
Upload
Overview. In our scenario, cloud user U continuously logs customer access and sends
logï¬les to the cloud. Each day, U starts using a new logï¬le. For simplicity, we assume
that entries logged by U are simple words. Each logï¬le is encrypted word by word using
a â€œstateful cipherâ€ EK, and resulting ciphertexts are written to a ï¬le, respectively. The
encrypted ï¬les are sent to the cloud.
Deï¬nition 3 (Stateful Cipher). Given standard symmetric encryption EK with key K,
e.g., AES, we extend E to a stateful cipher by adding â€œcountersâ€ Î³wi that count the
history of inputs wi. Each time E encrypts wi, counter Î³wi is increased by one.
In conclusion, a stateful cipher is a cipher that knows how often it has encrypted a
speciï¬c plaintext. The following presents one trivial stateful cipher construction used
in PRISM to encrypt before uploading.
Stateful Cipher Example (see Algorithm 1): For simplicity, user U uses a secret key
K to derive a different key for each day d, e.g., Kd := HMACK(d).
For each day, U maintains a hash table containing the list of counters Î³wi in Uâ€™s local
storage. At the beginning of each day, U initializes all counters to 0, i.e., Î³wi = 0. Now,
for each logentry wi that should be stored in the cloud, U computes Î³wi and increases
Î³wi by 1. Then, U computes ciphertext Ci := EKd(wi, Î³wi). User U sends ciphertext
Ci to the cloud that stores it in this dayâ€™s ï¬le. For the (AES) encryption EKd(wi, Î³wi),
â€œ,â€ denotes an unambiguouspairing of inputs. We discuss the reason for using a â€œstateful
cipherâ€ over using, e.g., a CBC mode of encryption in Section 5.1.
Summarizing, with respect to Deï¬nition 1, Encrypt in PRISM takes K to derive
a separate key Kd for each ï¬le to be encrypted. Actual encryption of each ï¬le is per-
formed word by word using the stateful cipher and key Kd, so E := {EL1, . . . , ELm}
where ELi := {EKi(w1, Î³w1), . . . , EKi(w|Li|, Î³w|Li|)}. Upload in PRISM can be re-
garded as simply sending the encrypted ï¬les E to MapReduce.
4.2
Search
User U wants to search a set of ï¬les for word w within a period of time. For ease of
understanding, we will restrict our description below to PRISM working on a single ï¬le

188
E.-O. Blass et al.
speciï¬ed by the user, i.e., the ï¬le of day d. With multiple ï¬les, all ï¬les will be separately
(but in parallel) processed with PRISM exactly like with a single ï¬le.
U sends map and reduce implementations of PRISM to MapReduce, and the map
phase starts. In the following, we describe the PRISM algorithms for, ï¬rst, the mappers
and in Section 14 the reducers. We would like to stress that the PRISM algorithms,
e.g., Java â€œ.classâ€ ï¬les, are not encrypted and not specially protected against a curious
cloud. Even though mappers and reducers know what operations they perform, they
cannot deduce any private information about stored data or details about the search.
Overview. Before scanning through its local InputSplit, a mapper node creates a matrix
with all elements initialized to â€œ0â€. PRISMâ€™s main idea is that while the mapper scans
the ciphertexts in its InputSplit, each ciphertext is assigned to one position, a certain
element in the matrix by computing a hash of the ciphertext. Additionally, for each
ciphertext, the mapper computes a single bit hash, and if the hash output bit is â€œ1â€, the
mapper puts a â€œ1â€ in the matrix at the assigned position. The idea is that user U can
also compute the position in the matrix and the one bit hash output for a word w he is
looking for. Roughly speaking, U now queries the mapper for the value of that bit in the
matrix using private information retrieval. If the bit retrieved from the mapper differs
from the bit computed by U, then U can decide, e.g., that w is not in this InputSplit.
Problem is that due to the limited size of the matrix and the properties of the hash
function, there might be collisions in the assignment process. That is, by chance there
can be two different ciphertexts being assigned with the same position in the matrix.
By chance, the bit retrieved by U can therefore be unrelated to w. This problem is
ampliï¬ed by the fact that U does not only receive a single bit for a single InputSplit,
but a combination (the sum) of all bits from all mappers working on InputSplits. To
mitigate this problem, PRISM repeats generation and ï¬lling of matrices a total of q
rounds. Also, setting an element in a matrix to â€œ1â€ depends on the round number. After
q rounds, the probability that the information U retrieved from this mapper is unrelated
to w therefore decreases, and U can ï¬nally decide whether w is inside this ï¬le.
Deï¬nition 4 (PIR Matrix). A binary tÃ—t matrix M with t = 2i, i âˆˆN is called a PIR
matrix. The mapper uses M to implicitly perform the privacy-preserving word search.
Deï¬nition 5 (Candidate Position). For each ciphertext Ci in an InputSplit, the can-
didate position (Xi, Yi) of Ci in M is computed by (Xi||Yi) := âŒŠCiâŒ‹2Â·log2 (t). Here,
âŒŠ. . .âŒ‹2Â·log2 (t) denotes truncation after 2 Â· log2 (t) bits. So, the ï¬rst log2 t bits of Ci de-
termine Xi, and the second log2 t bits determine Yi.
Deï¬nition 6 (PIR Input). If U is interested in a speciï¬c element (X, Y) in M, he
computes PIR input {Î±1, Î±2, . . . , Î±t}, where Î±X := b Â· (1 + aX Â· N) mod p, and
âˆ€i Ì¸= X, Î±i := b Â· (ai Â· N) mod p. Random values b and ai are chosen as for the
Trapdoor Group PIR scheme presented in Section 3.2.

4.3
Result Analysis
The only piece left is the Decode algorithm of Deï¬nition 1 which we will describe in
the following. For each outsourced ï¬le (day d), user U retrieves an output ï¬le generated

PRISM â€“ Privacy-Preserving Search in MapReduce
191
by reducers. Now, U analyzes retrieved ï¬lesâ€™ content to ï¬nally conclude which of the
outsourced ï¬les contain w (using S). Again for ease of understanding, we restrict our
description to the analysis of the result generated from PRISM on a single outsourced
ï¬le called FILE. U repeats this process with all other results from the other ï¬les accord-
ingly.
Deï¬nition 8 (Collision). Assume U is looking for w, so C := EKd(w, 1). Similar
to hash functions, a collision in PIR matrix M denotes the case of an event where
the candidate position (X â€², Yâ€²) of another ciphertext Câ€² Ì¸= C matches the candidate
position ( Ë†
X || Ë†Y) = âŒŠE(w, 1)âŒ‹2Â·log2 (t) of w in M. That is, âŒŠCâŒ‹2Â·log2 (t) = âŒŠCâ€²âŒ‹2Â·log2 (t).
Deï¬nition 9 (One-Collision). A one-collision is the event where in an InputSplit a ci-
phertext Câ€² Ì¸= EKd(w, 1) puts a 1 into the same candidate position in M as EKd(w, 1).
Overview: The rationale for the result analysis protocol of PRISM is to observe the can-
didate position of C over q rounds to mitigate the effect of one-collisions. Of particular
interest will be rounds where âŒŠh(C, i)âŒ‹1 = 1.
First, U un-blinds all values received from reducers. Based on the result, U distin-
guishes two cases.
Case 1.) If a reducer, reducing for a speciï¬c ï¬le FILE, has returned the value 0 for
Câ€™s candidate position, then U knows for sure that all mappers have output 0 for this
candidate position. Consequently, the candidate position in matrix M of each mapper
is 0. Therefore, C has not been in any of the InputSplits of FILE, and U reasons w Ì¸âˆˆ
FILE. If C would have been in one InputSplit, then at least the mapper working on this
InputSplit would have returned a 1 in this round.
Deï¬nition 10 (Contradiction). Let w be the word U is looking for, and C its cipher-
text. If in some round i, âŒŠh(C, i)âŒ‹1 = 1 holds, and the reducer for ï¬le FILE sends U a
value of 0 then this is called a contradiction.
In case of such a contradiction, U for sure knows that w is not in ï¬le FILE.
Case 2.) If, however, this reducer returns a value > 0, then w was in at least one
InputSplit or a one-collision has occurred in at least one InputSplit. User U can neither
decide w Ì¸âˆˆFILE nor w âˆˆFILE with absolute certainty.
Uâ€™s strategy is to keep the probability for one-collisions low and run multiple rounds
q, such that eventually a contradiction occurs (â‡’U decides w Ì¸âˆˆFILE), or, if no con-
tradiction occurs, U decides w âˆˆFILE with only a small error probability Perr.
Details: U executes Algorithm 3. For each ï¬le, U is only interested in row Ë†Y of matri-
ces M, as they can refer to candidate position ( Ë†
X , Ë†Y), only. Therefore, U keeps values
{sFILE, Ë†

In conclusion, Uâ€™s strategy can be summarized by: output w Ì¸âˆˆFILE, if âˆƒi, si = 0

Saving Computation: To save some computation in PRISM, we can modify the hash-
based mechanism that determines whether to put a â€œ1â€ or a â€œ0â€ in a certain element
in M. Recall that the ï¬rst 2 Â· log2(t) bit of a ciphertext C are used to determine its
position (element) in M. However, instead of computing an expensive hash function
âŒŠh(Ci, j)âŒ‹1 to get a single bit in round j, we can simply replace the hash and take Câ€™s
bit on position (2 Â· log2(t) + j). Assuming that cipher E has good security properties
(each bit of C is â€œ1â€ with probability 1
2), this results in the same property as using the
hash: eventually two different ciphertexts that collide in M will differ and lead to a
contradiction. We use this computation reduction in our evaluation in Section 6.
5
PRISM Analysis
5.1
Privacy
We will now show why PRISM is privacy-preserving. The main rationale behind our
proof is to show that pairs of output generated by both our stateful cipher construc-
tion EK(wi, Î³wi) (Section 4.1) and the PIR-based search mechanism (Section 4.2) are
computationally indistinguishable for A. Below, we assume a sufï¬ciently large security
parameter s and probabilistic polynomial time adversaries A.
Theorem 1. PRISM is a privacy-preserving cloud word search scheme assuming pseu-
dorandom properties for E and the trapdoor group property of the PIR scheme.
Proof (Sketch). Assume there would be an adversary A with Pr(GAME(A) = 1) >
1
2 + Ïµ(s), i.e., A has non-negligible advantage over guessing. As PRISM generates Eb
and Qb independently from each other, this would indicate that A has non-negligible
advantage over guessing in determining b from either Eb or Qb (or both).
We will now show with the following two lemmas that this is impossible.
Lemma 1. In PRISM, any pair of sequences of ciphertexts (ï¬les EL and ELâ€²) gen-
erated by a pair of sequences of words (ï¬les L and Lâ€²) is computationally indistin-
guishable for A, assuming E is a pseudorandom permutation and â€œ,â€ an unambiguous
pairing of inputs.
Proof (Sketch). First, note that our stateful-cipher uses a different random key for each
ï¬le. In a learning phase, A makes a number of queries to two stateful-cipher oracles
encrypting with two different keys K0, K1. Then, A prepares word w, submits to a
challenge oracle and gets back EKb(w, Î³b,w), b âˆˆ{0, 1}. A has to output b correctly
with only negligible advantage over guessing.
However, we now show by using the hybrid argument [28] that the distributions
generated by EKi(w, Î³i,w) are computationally indistinguishable for A. That is, pairs
EK0(w, Î³0,w), EK1(w, Î³1,w) are distinguishable with only negligible advantage over
guessing). As â€œ,â€ is an unambiguous pairing, we now write wâ€²
i instead of (w, Î³i,w).

(2.) - (3.) and (4.) - (5.): the probability to distinguish a random permutation from a

Lemma 2. Based on the trapdoor group assumption (â€œTGAâ€), PRISMâ€™s PIR-search
produces computationally indistinguishable pairs of queries Qi.
Proof (Sketch). Assume A submits two words w1, w2 to an oracle. The oracle picks
Ë†b âˆˆ{0, 1} and returns QË†b := {Î±Ë†b,1 := b Â· eË†b,1 mod p, . . . , Î±Ë†b,t := b Â· eË†b,t mod p},

However, we will now show that any pair of sequences of Î± values is computa-

the PIR protocol, based on TGA: for all adversaries A, Pr[A(b Â· e1, . . . , b Â· et) =
LSB(e1, . . . , et)] = Ïµ(s). That is, given b Â· ei, the probability that A computes low
order bits of ei mod N (â€œLSBâ€) is negligible [37].
Assume that A can distinguish sequences Q0 = {Î±0,i} and Q1 = {Î±1,i} with
non-negligible advantage. This would violate TGA as follows. First, note that besides
Î±0,X0, Î±1,X1 all elements in both sequences {Î±0,i} and {Î±1,i} are created in the same

distinguish between sequences {Î±0,i} and {Î±1,i}, then A can determine with non-ne-
gligible probability X0 or X1 and thus value i with ei = 1 mod N, violating TGA.

6
Evaluation
To show its real-world feasibility, we have implemented and evaluated PRISM with the
scenario described in the introduction. The source code is available for public down-
load [1]. We received 16 days of log data from May 2010 from a small local Internet
provider. This provider logs and retains all customersâ€™ DNS resolve requests for possi-
ble forensic analysis and intrusion detection. Log data is split into ï¬les on a daily basis.
Each ï¬le contains one day of logged 3-tuples: timestamp, customer IP (anonymized by
provider for regulatory matters), hostname. The scenario for our evaluation is to use
PRISM to upload this data encrypted to MapReduce and perform a search for speciï¬c
hostnames in a privacy-preserving manner. This is useful for, e.g., â€œpassive DNS anal-
ysisâ€ to determine at which day certain command-and-control centers of botnets have
been accessed by customer machines, cf., Bilge et al. [5]. The goal of our experiments
was to analyze the computational overhead induced by PRISMâ€™s privacy mechanism,
i.e., the additional time consumed by PRISM over non-privacy-preserving MapReduce.
6.1
Setup
For the 16 days, the log data contains â‰ˆ3Â·108 log entries, i.e., â‰ˆ2Â·107 per ï¬le/day. The
total space required by all ï¬les uploaded into MapReduce using PRISM is 27 GByte,
on average 1.7 GByte per ï¬le.
Our experiments have been performed on a small â€œcloudâ€ comprising 1 master com-
puter and 9 slaves. Computers featured a 2.5 GHz Pentium Dual Core and 4 GByte of
RAM, running a standard desktop installation of Fedora 11. With this hardware conï¬g-
uration, a total of 18 CPUs were available for maps and reduces. We installed Hadoop
version 0.20.2 on our cloud. Being aware that tailoring MapReduceâ€™s conï¬guration pa-
rameters can have a huge impact on performance, we use the standard, out-of-the-box
conï¬guration of Hadoop 0.20.2 without any conï¬guration tweaks. Performance tuning
is out of scope of this paper. Similarly, as the InputSplit size is recommended to be be-
tween 64 MByte and 128 MByte, we chose SInputSplit = 96 MByte (InputSplits must
be dividable by 3 Â· 32 Byte, since log entries are 3-tuples).

In addition to the evaluation with 96 MByte InputSplits, we also performed a second
measurement with larger InputSplits of 120 MByte. We expected a slightly improved
performance of PRISM due to the fact that for the larger ï¬les the total number of Input-
Splits c reduced to less than our 18 available CPUs. Therewith, no costly (re-)scheduling
takes places, and mappers do not have to process 2 InputSplits sequentially.
Finally, to put timing results into perspective, we implemented and measured a triv-
ial, non-privacy-preserving MapReduce search called Baseline. Baseline search con-
sists of an empty map phase, where mappers simply scan over InputSplits and compare
each word of the InputSplit with a predetermined one, but do not generate any key-value
pairs. Only at the end of the map phase, a single intermediate key-value pair per map-
per (e.g., â€œfoundâ€) is sent to reducers. Reducers discard this key-value pair and write
empty ï¬les to disk. This trivial baseline only serves in deducing the overhead implied
by PRISM, not taking MapReduce speciï¬c delays due to rescheduling, speculative exe-
cution of backup tasks etc. [17, 34] into account. Note that linear scanning through the
entire InputSplit is mandatory, as we assume our data to be unordered and unsorted.
For the private information retrieval algorithm, we set m = 400 as suggested by
Trostle and Parrish [37] for good security. Our Java implementation is a naive, straight-
forward implementation using Javaâ€™s BigInteger without any performance optimiza-
tions. As symmetric encryption cipher, we used AES with 256 Bit blocksize from the
GNU Crypto Library V2.0.1 [20]. As individual DNS entries occurred way less than
216 times per day, we reserved |Î³| = 2 Bytes and truncated entries longer than 30 Byte
down to the last 30 Byte. Because the size of input |wi|+|Î³wi| is less than Eâ€™s blocksize
(using standard padding for wi), concatenation provides an unambiguous pairing.
Simulating U, we computed n and c using blocksize, InputSplit size SInputSplit,,
and individual ï¬le size SFile. Assuming that U targets an error probability of Perr <
0.01, we derived t and q. Table 1 summarizes parameters (t, q) computed for each ï¬le
individually. Compared to q, we observed that parameter t has a much higher impact
on Perr, but a comparatively lower impact on computations. Therefore, we increased
preferably t than q. Higher values for (t, q) will achieve even smaller values for Perr,
but Table 1 shows the computationally â€œcheapestâ€ combination of (t, q).
6.2

Computational overhead at the cloud is low as indicated by Figure 1 (PRISMâ€™s timing
results). We have sorted the 16 ï¬les based on their size in an increasing order, i.e, the
size of the smallest log ï¬le we received from the Internet provider was 0.45 GByte, the
largest one was 2.25 GByte. PRISMâ€™s execution time was clocked on each ï¬le 6 times,
respectively, and Fig. 1 shows the average. For each ï¬le, Fig. 1 shows two stacked
boxes, respectively: the ï¬rst one for 96 MByte and the second one for 120 MByte

Fig. 1. Wall clock timings for PRISM and Baseline, with SInputSplit = 96 and 120 MByte
InputSplit size. Each of the stacked boxes comprises, ï¬rst, the baseline timing and,
second, the additional time required to run PRISM. To give trust into the evaluation,
Fig. 1 also shows 95% conï¬dence intervals drawn right next to each box.
Timings shown in Fig. 1 are â€œwall clockâ€ timings. This captures the complete time
elapsed from submitting the PRISM map and reduce classes and starting the job until
the end of the reduce phase. In the real-world, wall clock time reï¬‚ects the time a cloud,
e.g., Amazon [2], would charge a user U. In conclusion, the additional overhead over
the trivial Baseline MapReduce jobs was on average 11% with a 95% conï¬dence inter-
val of Â±3. The largest overhead seen was 24% over Baseline. This overhead is mostly
computational overhead, as there is no difference in disk access between Baseline and
PRISM and network volume increases only little by sending slightly larger values dur-
ing â€œReduceâ€. These results do not only show the feasibility of PRISM in practice, but
also demonstrate the low overhead implied by PRISM over the non-privacy preserving
MapReduce job. We claim that a performance optimized (not based on Java BigInteger)
implementation improves performance signiï¬cantly and furthermore reduces overhead.
The simple increase from 96 MByte InputSplit size to 120 MByte InputSplit size
has reduced wall clock times for MapReduce jobs by 9% on average (95% conï¬dence
interval of Â±4). Files of size smaller than 2 GByte are split into â‰¤18 InputSplits, and
both jobs, PRISM and Baseline, are processed completely in parallel. This indicates
that a careful conï¬guration of Hadoop MapReduceâ€™s many system parameters, hand-
crafted and speciï¬c to the scenario and jobs to be executed, will lead to substantial
performance improvements. This also indicates that in a cloud with more CPUs than
in our small setup, the increased number of CPUs will enable to conï¬gure way smaller
InputSplits being processed in parallel. Substantially smaller InputSplits will be beneï¬-
cial for the overall performance of PRISM or any MapReduce job. However, increasing
the number of InputSplits also implies a performance penalty due to (re-)scheduling
and coordination activities of the central job tracker, cf., Pavlo et al. [34], so a trade-off
has to be found. MapReduce conï¬guration optimizations are, however, out of scope.

PRISM â€“ Privacy-Preserving Search in MapReduce
197
To better understand the cloudâ€™s computational overhead, we also measured the com-
putation time for a PRISM mapper. On a single CPU, execution of an isolated
PRISM map function on a single InputSplit is ca. 9 times slower than Baseline (9.3
for 96 MByte and 9.1 for 120 MByte, 95% conï¬dence interval of Â±0.1). While this
seems to be a lot, we remark that 1.) this map overhead is constant for an InputSplit and
does not depend on or scale with the total size of the data, 2.) there is a lot of potential
to improve our map implementation, 3.) this overhead is obviously amortized by other
MapReduce aspects such as the Reduce phase and also disk latency, network overhead
etc., and 4.) a user is charged for the total system time, i.e., the wall clock time.
Computational overhead at the User is also low in PRISM: per ï¬le, the preparation
of, e.g., 212 Î± values for the underlying PIR scheme is barely measurable (â‰ˆ200 ms) on
a PC with 2.5 GHz CPU. During result analysis, U automatically discards all received
values that he is not interested in, i.e., all besides sFILE,Y,1â‰¤iâ‰¤q. For these q values, a
total of q Java BigInteger multiplications with modulo have to be performed. For our
examples with q â‰¤100, this was not measurable at less than 1 ms.
Memory consumption for U is, on the one hand, constant; U only stores the 256 bit
AES key K. On the other hand however, the cloud user Uâ€™s memory consumption scales
linearly with O(Î£), i.e., the number of different words. This is due to the construction
of our stateful cipher that stores counters Î³ in a hashtable. In our straightforward im-
plementation with Javaâ€™s standard Hashtable, memory consumption of this hashtable
was 548 MByte for the largest log ï¬le. While this is certainly a lot of RAM, we con-
jecture this to be available on PC hardware â€“ moreover, as there is a large potential for
performance tuning with such data structures.
Communication overhead for PRISM is dominated by the underlying PIR scheme.
U sends, besides .class ï¬les once, only the t Î± values per ï¬le to the cloud. For example,
with t = 212 and m = 400 Bit, this computes to 200 KByte per ï¬le. The response
from the cloud is, for each round, t values of size m. The most expensive conï¬guration
in terms of communication in our experiments has been t = 210, q = 100; this results
in â‰ˆ5 MByte communication overhead. Note that communication complexity in the
underlying PIR scheme by Trostle and Parrish [37] is linear in the square root of the
total table size, i.e., O(t). This can be further reduced by using recursive PIR queries
to O(tÏµ), for any Ïµ > 0 [29]. Those optimizations as well as amortization techniques
discussed by Ostrovsky and Skeith [33] are out of scope.
In conclusion, PRISM is very lightweight for a user using standard PC hardware.
Discussion: On a larger cluster in a more professional environment (hundreds or
even thousands of CPUs [25]), all ï¬les will be processed in parallel. As shown in Fig. 1,
total time for the 2 GByte ï¬le is â‰ˆ350 s. However, already â‰ˆ340 s are required by
MapReduce just to â€œscanâ€ through the various InputSplits, see Baseline. Such inefï¬-
ciency with non-optimal conï¬gurations has been observed before, and our results are
along the lines of Pavlo et al. [34]. Here, a â€œgrepâ€-like MapReduce job on 1 TByte of
data took â‰ˆ1, 500 s on 50 CPUs which would be â‰ˆ20 times faster than our Base-
line. However, Pavlo et al. [34] use a slightly tuned conï¬guration and moreover a more
efï¬cient scanning through InputSplits (100 Byte text values instead of 32 Byte binary
values in our case) which is known to lead to signiï¬cant performance increases [27].

198
E.-O. Blass et al.
7
Related Work
Private Information Retrieval: Private Information Retrieval (and similarly oblivious
transfer and oblivious RAM) has received a lot of attention [9, 13, 19, 22, 29, 32, 33,
35]. In PIR, a user retrieves a speciï¬c data from a database. The only â€œprivacyâ€ goal
in PIR is access privacy whereby the server should not discover which data a user is
interested in. Note that PIR does not ensure privacy of data in the database. PRISM,
however, focuses on searching for a word and uses PIR only as a tool.
Searchable Encryption: With searching on encrypted data techniques [6], user pri-
vacy is guaranteed thanks to the encryption of the queries and the stored data. However,
PRISM offers higher privacy guarantees since in existing searchable encryption solu-
tions [4, 6â€“8, 10, 16, 21, 31, 36], the result (â€œfoundâ€ or â€œnot foundâ€) originating from a
query is known to the adversary; therefore as opposed to PRISM, standard searchable
encryption techniques do not ensure query privacy. Moreover, existing mechanisms
cannot be easily extended to leverage from a parallelized cloud setup: while in theory
the search on encrypted data itself could be run in parallel on subsets of data, todayâ€™s
solution do not support the combination (aggregation) of results (as in a reduce phase).
To conclude, PRISM not only ensures both storage privacy and query privacy, but also
enables the aggregation of results originating from intermediate parallelized operations.
8
Conclusion
PRISM is the ï¬rst privacy-preserving search scheme suited for cloud computing. That
is, PRISM provides storage and query privacy while introducing only limited overhead.
PRISM is speciï¬cally designed to leverage parallelism and efï¬ciency of the MapRe-
duce paradigm. Moreover, PRISM is compatible with any standard MapReduce-based
cloud infrastructure (such as Amazonâ€™s), and does not require modiï¬cations to the un-
derlying system. Thanks to this compatibility, PRISM has been efï¬ciently implemented
on an experiemental cloud computing environment using Hadoop MapReduce. Besides
a throughout analysis, performance of PRISM has been evaluated on that environment
through search operations in DNS logs provided by an ISP. PRISMâ€™s overhead over
non-privacy-preserving search is only 11% on average, acertaining its efï¬ciency.
